And in our case: The name "Chassidim"—a name by which a special type of Bnei Yisrael are called in Shas and the Midrashim of Chazal—was established in the mouths of all Yisrael without distinction of party specifically for the students of the Baal Shem Tov, the Maggid, the Alter Rebbe, etc.

The Rebbe Rayatz once said, that seemingly, the Chassidim should have been called by the name by which their opponents are called, and the opponents should have been called by the name Chassidim, for the Chassidim are those who came with *new things* (to which there was opposition), and in practice, the opponents themselves called the Chassidim by the name "Chassidim" and themselves by the name "Mitnagdim"! Rather, everything is by Divine Providence, such that with the revelation of the Torah of Chassidus, *everyone admitted* and called it by the name "*Chassidus*" and those who walk in it by the name "*Chassidim*," and "once he has spoken, he does not retract."

Chassidus is not a matter of a party, as the words of the Rebbe Rayatz that the Alter Rebbe founded the Torah of Chassidus for all of Yisrael, and since this was the will of the Alter Rebbe, it will certainly be so in practice,

—for a tzaddik is the master to fulfill his wishes, as the saying goes "A tzaddik decrees and Hashem fulfills," and furthermore, "Even if Hashem decrees a decree, he (the tzaddik) annuls it"—

And certainly powers are given from above to make all Bnei Yisrael into Chassidim, and the prophecy "May your springs be spread outward" will already be fulfilled.

The "outward"—of Torah in general and of the Torah of Chassidus in particular—is present; the "springs" are present; only the "spreading" is missing. We must therefore go out and awaken and bring Jews closer to Chassidus.

***

**Q.** In continuation of the above—I would like to mention again what was discussed on Shabbos Bereishis regarding the proposal *that everyone* should strive until 19 Kislev to influence ten (a minyan) of Bnei Yisrael, in thought, in speech, or in action, or in all three together.

There are those who think that the words are not directed at them. — Everyone agrees that one must work in strengthening Judaism in general and in spreading Chassidus

#### 79

in particular, but the argument is: The words are not directed at me. And similarly regarding the aforementioned proposal: many—I do not want to say most—think that the words are not directed at them. This is a correct proposal, but, let someone else do it.

There are those who think that this matter belongs to simple people, but they stand above it. And there are those who are not mistaken to think that they stand above it, and their argument is the opposite: in such a matter, people who are above them should be occupied, whereas they do not belong to influencing others, and they barely manage with themselves.

The truth is—that both arguments are based on a mistake.

The Rebbe Rashab writes that now we do not know the secret of the clarifications, and everyone is obligated to occupy himself with all the matters of clarification that come his way from above, and since from above they give him powers, this is proof that they demand this from above, for "everything that Hashem created in His world, He did not create one thing in vain." And another matter greater than this: if he does not do this, he is not utilizing the powers that were given to him, and thereby he makes—in the creation of Hashem—an innovation for the worse, a thing in vain.

**R.** And it should be added, that the matter required of him will certainly come to fruition even without him. The Jew who needs to be brought closer through him, will be brought closer even without him. And certainly the general goal "May your springs be spread outward" will be fulfilled in practice even without him. However, he must strive—*for his own sake*—to carry out the matter.

—Someone wrote to me that he has a son who has reached his time, but, he does not want to do anything for the sake of a shidduch, because he fears that perhaps he will not aim correctly ("he will not find"), and his desire is to wait until they come to him with a shidduch proposal ready-made, and then he will see that this is from Heaven. And it is understood, that this approach is the opposite of what is desired, and as Chazal said "It is the way of a man to seek out..." and "Lest another precede him, through mercy."

And similarly in our case—that he must fear "lest another precede him through mercy," and therefore he must—for his own sake—want and do everything in his power to fulfill the matter and influence others.

***

#### 80

**S.** In continuation of what was discussed above regarding the matter of "Go for yourself"—there is a maamar of the Rebbe Rayatz from Shabbos Parshas Lech Lecha 5686 (twenty years from the year 5666), and he brings there the words of Chazal "Four things tear up a person's decree: tzedakah, crying out, changing the name, and changing the action... and some say also changing the place, as it is written 'And Hashem said to Avram, Go for yourself, etc.'," and he concludes according to the opinion of the Ran that these things are effective even for a decree that came through sin and iniquity.

And it can be said their meaning in spiritual avodah:

"Tzedakah"—the matter of tefillah which is included in acts of kindness, as Rashi explains that "the contemplation of tefillah is included in acts of kindness." And as explained in Chassidus in the explanation of the words of Chazal "Whoever says he has only Torah, etc., even Torah he does not have," but rather there must be "Torah and acts of kindness," that the meaning is tefillah.

"Crying out"—the matter of surrender and giving, and not according to logic and reason.

"Changing the action"—regarding us in particular—the study of Chassidus for oneself, and those who have already studied will begin to study more, and the students of the yeshiva will study not only in the yeshiva schedule, but also with addition, and for themselves.

And through the aforementioned changing of action there will be automatically also "changing of place"—a spiritual place which means (as the Rambam writes) elevation and level, that is, a change in his status and level.

And through changing the place there will also be "changing the name"—that he will receive the true name of "Chassid."

—The Rebbe Rayatz would say, that there are those who think that by snapping their fingers in tefillah, and understanding "something" in the Torah of Chassidus, they become a "Chassid." A "Chassid" is one who studies Chassidus for himself, davens at length, and keeps the study of the shiurim.

Through changing the action, which also leads to changing the place, changing the level, there will automatically be a changing of the name—that they will be able to be called "Chassidim," Chabad Chassidim, who,

#### 81

to them was given the land in order to carry out in it the matter of "May your springs be spread outward," the victory of Chassidus, which is the matter of 19 Kislev.

And through this that they will complete the aforementioned matter until 19 Kislev, then, just as on the first 19 Kislev there was the victory of Chassidus, "ours is the victory," so too will be fulfilled on this 19 Kislev the "ours is the victory," and soon and in our days the Rebbe will lead us toward Moshiach, amen, may it be His will.

**********

## Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Chayei Sarah, Mevarchim Chodesh Kislev, 5711

#### 82

**A.** The day of Shabbos includes all the days of the coming week. At times, we find that Shabbos includes only the first three days, while the three days that follow belong to the subsequent Shabbos[1], and at times we find that Shabbos includes all the days of the coming week[2].

Based on the statement that Shabbos includes all the days of the week, it follows that this Shabbos also includes Rosh Chodesh Kislev[3].

**B.** The reason it is called *Rosh* Chodesh (Head of the Month) rather than "the beginning of the month" is the same as the reason regarding Rosh Hashanah, which is called *Rosh* Hashanah (Head of the Year) rather than "the beginning of the year."

It is explained in Chassidus[4] that "Rosh Hashanah" is not merely the beginning of the year, but also the "head" of the year. Just as the head contains, first and foremost, the vitality of all the limbs, and even after the vitality of the limbs is drawn forth from the head, it remains connected to the vitality of the head and the brain[5], so too does Rosh Hashanah contain the vitality of all the days of the year. Even after these days are drawn forth, they must remain connected to their head — Rosh Hashanah.

Its application in *avodah* is that throughout the entire year, the awakening one experienced on Rosh Hashanah must be evident ("*es darf zich hern*"). This is especially true regarding the matter of *kabbalas ol* (accepting the yoke), the primary focus of Rosh Hashanah — "the mitzvah of the day is the Shofar"[6], the essence of which is the acceptance of His sovereignty, may He be blessed. As our Sages said[7], "Say before Me on Rosh Hashanah... *Malchiyos* (verses of sovereignty), so that you will make Me King over you... and with what? With the Shofar." This must be done in a way that "His awe shall be upon you"[8].

The same applies to Rosh Chodesh — it is not merely the beginning of the month, but

#### 83

also the "head" of the month, which contains the vitality of all the days of the month, and from which the days of the month receive their vitality even afterward.

**C.** One may add to the explanation of the general concept of Rosh Chodesh: in certain details, it serves as an intermediary between the weekdays and Shabbos.

On Rosh Chodesh, work is permitted, and it contains all the activities of the weekdays, unlike Shabbos and Yom Tov. Nevertheless, we find in the Tanach[9] that Rosh Chodesh is not counted among the other weekdays, but is counted separately. The reason for this is that it is not like the other weekdays; rather, it is on a higher plane. This is why we recite Hallel on it and offer the *musaf* sacrifice—an addition to all other days. As explained in Chassidus[10], the superiority of Rosh Chodesh over the other weekdays is like the superiority of Olam HaYetzirah, or even Olam HaBeriah, over Olam HaAsiyah.

The concept here is as follows:

The language of the verse regarding the performance of work during the six weekdays is: "Six days *you shall work*"[11]. The word "you shall work" (*ta'avod*) also implies a *command*. Although the simple interpretation is that on the six weekdays it is *permitted* to perform work, it is explained in Chassidus[12] that, as the literal meaning of the verse suggests, on the six weekdays one *must* perform work. This is also somewhat implied by the language of the Midrash Rabbah[13]: "To work it—six days you shall work."

The reason for the obligation of work during the six weekdays is that through engaging in work according to the Torah, one accomplishes above the matter of "For six days Hashem made"[14]—the drawing down of a Divine illumination to bring into being and animate the worlds, a Divine illumination that is clothed within nature[15].

In contrast, on Shabbos, work is forbidden, because Shabbos is above the matter of "For six days Hashem made," as on it shines a Divine illumination that is not clothed and hidden within the garments of nature.

#### 84

And Rosh Chodesh—its superiority over the other weekdays is that *nature* itself is more elevated.

In other words: Shabbos is a mode of conduct that is above nature; the weekdays are a mode of conduct that is according to nature; and Rosh Chodesh is a mode of conduct that draws and infuses the "above nature" into nature.

**D.** Based on this, we can explain the recitation of Hallel on Rosh Chodesh, which is not done on other weekdays, nor on Shabbos:

Hallel speaks of a mode of miraculous conduct. Therefore, we do not recite Hallel on weekdays (to the extent that "one who recites Hallel every day is a blasphemer"[16]), because on those days, conduct must be specifically according to nature, as it is written[17], "And Hashem your G-d will bless you in all that you do," and "we do not rely on miracles"[18]. On Shabbos, we do not recite Hallel because on Shabbos "you shall do no work"[19], and "he who toils on Erev Shabbos shall eat on Shabbos"[20] as a matter of course.

On Rosh Chodesh, which is the intermediary between Shabbos and the weekdays, we recite Hallel—out of a request to Hashem to draw and infuse the conduct that is above nature into the work and activities of the weekdays[21].

However, the recitation of Hallel on Rosh Chodesh (as well as the prohibition of work for women on Rosh Chodesh[22]) is a matter of custom, whereas on Yom Tov and Chanukah, the recitation of Hallel is a matter of law[23]—because on Rosh Chodesh, the miraculous conduct permeates the weekday activities in such a way that the weekday activities are visibly seen, while the miracle is hidden. This is unlike Yom Tov and Chanukah, where the miracle is revealed.

**E.** More specifically, it is explained at length in Chassidus[24] that there is a superiority of Rosh Chodesh over Shabbos, and of Shabbos over Rosh Chodesh:

#### 85

The superiority of Shabbos over Rosh Chodesh is that on Rosh Chodesh work is permitted and there are weekday activities, whereas on Shabbos work is forbidden, as it is entirely above the weekday.

And from this very aspect lies the superiority of Rosh Chodesh over Shabbos—that because work is permitted, the miraculous conduct is drawn and infused specifically into the weekday activities.

One can explain the superiority of Rosh Chodesh over Shabbos by way of the superiority of Purim over the three festivals—that specifically the joy of Purim is "until one does not know"[25], a thing we do not find on other festivals, for although their mitzvah is in joy, one does not continue with wine and joy, etc.[26].

This is prefaced by the words of the Gemara[27] explaining why we do not recite Hallel on Purim—one of the answers is because "we are still servants of Achashverosh."

In other words: the miracle of Purim was clothed in nature, as they had to request and act through Achashverosh so that he would annul the decree, and even afterward they remained servants of Achashverosh; therefore, we do not recite Hallel on it.

And although on one hand there is a deficiency in a miracle clothed in nature (which is why we do not recite Hallel for it), on the other hand, there is a superiority in it—for its being clothed in nature indicates the greatness of the Divine light that permeates nature itself. For this reason, the joy of Purim is greater than the joy of all the festivals[28].

The same is the superiority of Rosh Chodesh over Shabbos—that the miraculous conduct is drawn and infused specifically into the conduct of nature and weekday activities.

**F.** One should add and explain the reason for the custom of the prohibition of work on Rosh Chodesh specifically for women:

Among the differences between women and men is that women are analogous to the body, and men are analogous to the soul[29].

Therefore, women, who are analogous to the body that is clothed and immersed in the affairs of the world, require a safeguard ("*a bavarenish*") and a sign, through the abstention from

#### 86

work on Rosh Chodesh. In contrast, men, who are analogous to the soul that is above the affairs of the world—and whose purpose, on the contrary, is to effect the refinement and conquest of the world ("it is the way of a man to conquer"[30])—do not require such a safeguard and sign.

The lesson and moral for us is that we must be in a standing and state where we do not require safeguards and signs from matters that do not pertain to us. This is especially true through the connection to an incomparably lofty matter, to which one is attached, in a way that it becomes like *something tangible* for him; then he does not require peripheral proofs from the outside.

An example of this is regarding the connection to the Rebbe: Once, someone asked a Chossid if his Rebbe had *Ruach HaKodesh*. He replied: "What does that matter so much? I know he is a Rebbe, and either way: if a Rebbe needs *Ruach HaKodesh*—he has it, and if not—what is the excitement about *Ruach HaKodesh*?"[31].

This is similar to what the Rambam writes[32] regarding belief in Moshe Rabbeinu—that "the Israelites did not believe in him because of the signs he performed," but rather, "at the standing at Mount Sinai, where our eyes saw and not a stranger, etc."

— The Rebbe Rayatz related[33] that in his youth, when he studied in *cheder*, he asked his teacher, R' Shimshon[34], how is it possible that after they showed Pharaoh wonders and gave him plagues, it did not help, and he would repeatedly renege? The teacher gave him various answers, but they were not accepted by him. Finally, the teacher told him: "Pharaoh did not believe, and therefore, even when he saw wonders, when there was relief, he returned to his old ways, to his nature and his previous place. In contrast, Moshe was a Chossid, and specifically a Chabadnik, and therefore he did not seek wonders. He knew and believed immediately (that 'with our young and with our old... we will go'[35]) without wonders. His desire was to bring the Torah, the knowledge of Elokus. And accordingly, he also estimated regarding the Bnei Yisrael that their question would be 'What is His name?'[36], and when he would have an answer for that, then the Bnei Yisrael would accept him (without wonders)."[37].

**G.** In addition to what was said above regarding the general matter of Rosh Chodesh, there is

#### 87

a special superiority regarding this Rosh Chodesh—Rosh Chodesh *Kislev*—which includes all the *special days* of the month of Kislev:

9 Kislev—the birthday and the day of passing of the Mitteler Rebbe.

10 Kislev—the day of the liberation of the Mitteler Rebbe.

19 Kislev—the *Yom Hillula Rabba* (as termed by our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe[38]) of the Maggid, and the day of the liberation of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe.

24 Kislev—the renewal of the foundation of the Second Beis Hamikdash[39].

And 25 Kislev—on which the work of the Mishkan was completed, and afterward Hashem repaid him with the dedication of the Chashmonaim, the miracle of Chanukah in the Second Beis Hamikdash[40].

The beginning for all these matters is through the drawing down of the blessing on this Shabbos, from which "all the days are blessed," all the days of the week, and including Rosh Chodesh Kislev, which includes all of the above.

***

**H.** Regarding the verse "And Sarah died in Kiryat Arba, which is Chevron, and Avraham came to eulogize Sarah and to weep for her" in this week's *parsha*[41]—it is stated in the Zohar[42] that Sarah alludes to the body and Avraham alludes to the soul[43].

More specifically: "And Sarah died—this is the body; in Kiryat Arba—these are the four elements; which is Chevron—that they were connected in his body during his lifetime... And Avraham came—this is the soul; to eulogize Sarah—this is the body" (as explained there that even after death there is still a connection of the soul with the body)[44].

This interpretation—that Sarah alludes to the body and Avraham to the soul—must be consistent ("*oysgehalten*") throughout the entire Torah. Seemingly, one must understand: how can this be interpreted according to what was said to Avraham[45] (who alludes to the soul), "Whatever Sarah (who alludes to the body) says to you, *listen to her voice*"?

#### 88

**I.** This will be understood by prefacing the well-known teaching of the Baal Shem Tov[46] on the verse[47], "If you see the donkey of your enemy, etc."—regarding the *avodah with the body* specifically:

"*If you see the donkey* (*chamor*)"—when you look with careful scrutiny at your materiality (*chomer*), which is the body, you will see—"*of your enemy*"—that it hates the soul that yearns for Elokus and spirituality. For, at the beginning of a person's life and at the beginning of *avodah*, the body and the soul hate each other.

"*Crouching under its burden*"—"burden" (*masa*) refers to the yoke of Torah and Mitzvos, and this burden is "its burden" (*masa'o*), the burden of the *body*. This is why the Mitzvos were given to souls in bodies specifically, because the intent is to *refine the body*. Nevertheless, it is considered by it a "burden," and it does not desire it, and consequently it is "crouching under its burden."

"*And you shall refrain from helping it*"—you might think that since the body is "crouching under its burden," you should occupy yourself with the *avodah* that pertains to the soul, while you break the body through fasts and afflictions—to this it is said: "*You shall surely help with it*"—because one must refine and purify the body specifically.

**J.** The concept here is:

In addition to the fact that the Mitzvos were given to souls in bodies, the Mitzvos themselves were clothed in physical things specifically.

As is emphasized regarding the act (body) of the Mitzvah and the intent (soul) of the Mitzvah—that the essence is the *physical* act of the Mitzvah[48]. Therefore, even if one intends all the *kavanos* of the Mitzvah of Tefillin, but does not put on Tefillin in practice, he has not fulfilled the Mitzvah of Tefillin (and furthermore, he has even committed a transgression—that he neglected the Mitzvah of Tefillin). Conversely, if he put on Tefillin in practice and did not intend the *kavanos* (whether because he does not know the *kavanos*, or he knows but did not intend)—although he will receive punishment ("*m'vet im shmeisen*") for the lack of intent, nevertheless, he has fulfilled the Mitzvah itself.

— For a *Bris Milah* of one of the grandchildren of the Tzemach Tzedek, they had to choose between two *mohalim*: one was older, possessing knowledge of the writings of the Arizal [in all of them or not in all of them, but he knew the matters pertaining to *milah*], and the second was young, an expert in the craft of *milah*, but much simpler than the older one. The Tzemach Tzedek instructed to take the young one, saying that the essence is the act of cutting *in physicality* ("*m'darf poshut in gashmius opshneiden*")[49].

#### 89

And not only practical Mitzvos, but also Mitzvos that are duties of the heart, such as the love of Hashem and the fear of Hashem, or duties of the mind, such as the Mitzvah of believing in the Unity—their Mitzvah is that they be felt *in the physical flesh* of the heart and the brain[50].

Furthermore: just as in physicality "a good report makes the bones fat"[51], as is related in the Gemara[52] that hearing good news caused a change in the physicality of the body, so too must it be regarding the love of Hashem—that the feeling of "the closeness of G-d is good for me"[53] must be evident also in the flesh of the body. And likewise regarding the fear of Hashem: not only that a contraction occurs in the brain or even in the heart, but that the fear is evident also in the flesh of the body.

One can bring an example of this from the conduct of the *Nesi'im*—who showed us everything:

The story is known regarding our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, that when he reached the recitation of "*Uv'chen ten pachdecha*"—in the prayer of Rosh Hashanah or Yom Kippur—he rolled on the floor while saying *pach*... *pach*... because from the intensity of the fear he could not finish the word, and only after some time did he finish the word: "*pachdecha*."

And another example from the Rebbe the Tzemach Tzedek[54], that in the first years of his *nesius* he once sat and *farbrenged* with Chassidim, and took a drink called "Kavit" (a very strong drink called here "Neintziger"), one cup, a second cup, and he ordered a third cup to be poured. After a while, he passed his hand over his forehead, and the effect of the drink was not evident on him. Afterward he explained that he contemplated the greatness of Hashem until fear fell upon him, and "strong wine is dispelled by fear"[55]—meaning, that the fear acted upon the flesh of the body, to such an extent that through it, the effect of the drink departed from the body.

And the same regarding love—as is known[56] that the Tzaddik R' Menachem Nachum of Chernobyl was fat in his body from the recitation of *Amen Yehei Shmei Rabba*, for when he contemplated the greatness of Hashem, "*Shmei Rabba*," that He is "blessed forever and to all eternity," which is drawn into all the worlds, and including also in the physical world of *Asiyah*, then he was awakened with love and *taanug* so much that it even caused fatness in his body.

**K.** Based on this, one can explain what is written, "Whatever Sarah says to you, listen to her voice"—according to the interpretation of the Zohar that "Sarah" alludes to the body:

#### 90

The essence of the intent is in the body, but currently this is hidden, and in the future the superiority of the body will be revealed, to such an extent that the soul will be nourished by the body[57].

And since the Patriarchs "Hashem gave them a taste in this world of the World to Come"[58], the *superiority of the body* was felt by them even relative to the soul. Therefore, it was said to Avraham (who alludes to the soul), "Whatever *Sarah* (who alludes to the *body*) says to you, listen to her voice."

***

**L.** The aforementioned point that the essence of the intent is *in the body* is also emphasized in the order of conduct regarding recruitment to the army—that the essence of the examination is regarding the *physical* state, without considering the mental state (the state of the soul) in and of itself, but rather what also pertains to the physical state. Thus, if from the perspective of his mental state he is not among those who have the signs of a fool... (such as "one who sleeps in a cemetery, etc."[59]), and the body is healthy—then they take him to the army.

And to add, that not only does the state of the soul in and of itself not matter in recruitment to the army, but on the contrary, sometimes it is even a disadvantage, as the saying goes[60], "the strength of the soul is the weakness of the body."

One should also note the ruling[61], "Whoever accepts upon himself the yoke of Torah, they remove from him the yoke of government and the yoke of worldly affairs," meaning, that through the acceptance of the yoke of Torah, a "strength of the soul" is created in him, and consequently also a "weakness of the body," as our Sages said[62] that the Torah is called "*oz v'tushiyah*," *oz*—for the *Nefesh Elokis*, and *tushiyah*—that it weakens the strength of the *Nefesh HaBahamis*.

[In the aforementioned ruling ("Whoever accepts upon himself the yoke of Torah, they remove from him, etc.") no condition is stated regarding *the time* of the acceptance of the yoke of Torah, so that even if they have already called him to go to the army in two or three days, this is not an absolute thing, and if he accepts upon himself—*now*—the yoke of Torah, then they will remove from him the yoke of government and the yoke of worldly affairs[63]].

The reason that in the call to the army in physicality the mental state does not matter as much as the physical state is because such is the order in spirituality, *in the armies of Hashem*:

#### 91

The essence of the *avodah* of fulfilling the Mitzvos is the act in practice, "the act is the essence"[64], which is connected with the body, and not as much the spiritual intent connected with the soul, as mentioned above (section 10) that when one intends all the *kavanos* without the act in practice, he has not fulfilled his obligation, whereas when he fulfills the Mitzvah in the act in practice, even without *kavanos*, they take him to the army of Hashem.

One must only be careful of the things that are signs of a fool, such as "one who sleeps in a cemetery," which in spirituality means that during the day he is occupied with Torah and Mitzvos, but at night, when his soul ascends above, he leaves the body to sleep in the cemetery, the place of the *kelipos* which are called dead[65]. And likewise, "one who loses everything that is given to him"[66], which in spirituality means that he loses the level of *Mah*—the power of *bitul* and *kabbalas ol*—that is given to him[67].

**M.** This matter pertains especially to the students of the Yeshiva:

There are students who search and come to ask for advice so that they will have *desire* for the study of Torah.

The instruction for this is from the order of recruitment to the army in physicality—that they do not ask if he has a desire to go to the army or not (and if he does not have a desire, he should return home to rest, "*oysshlafen zich*," until he has a desire, and then he will go to the army), but rather, if he is only healthy, he goes to the army even if he has no desire. And the same regarding the army in spirituality—it does not matter if he has a desire (which is connected with the soul), but he must do what is incumbent upon him (and in this case—the study of Torah) out of *kabbalas ol*.

And on the contrary—in the study of Torah out of *kabbalas ol* there is a *superiority* over the study of Torah out of desire:

The story is known[68] that one of the Chassidim entered to the Tzemach Tzedek and complained that he had no desire to study, and the Tzemach Tzedek replied to him: "If so—that you have no desire—*it is a good thing*; what shall I do, *that I have a desire* to study!"

The explanation for this is according to what is explained in the *Hemshech* of 5666[69] regarding the greatness of the *superiority of toil* in Torah, that specifically through this one comes to the level of *Eden*, as it is written[70], "No eye has seen, O G-d, except for You, what You will do for him who waits for Him," "those *who toil* in the words of wisdom"[71]. Therefore,

#### 92

one should rejoice that he has no desire to study, because in such a state he will need to *toil more* in the study of Torah out of *kabbalas ol*, and through this he will be able to come to the greatness of the level of *Eden*, etc.

It turns out that a student who searches and asks for advice so that he will have desire in study is *waiving* a wonderful opportunity ("*a gevaldige meglechkeit*") that fell to his lot:

He "merited" that he has no desire and does not want to study ("*er vill nit, un vill nit shtark, un gor shtark*"...), and consequently he has the possibility to study out of *kabbalas ol* and toil, and to merit the level of *Eden*. And nevertheless, he waives "Gan Eden," "Eden," and all the matters, and in exchange for this he asks... that he should have a desire to study!

— When he asks for a desire to study, *they will give him* what he asks, because Hashem is good and does good, and gives to each one according to his will and desire, as the *Metzudos* writes[72] in the interpretation of the verse[73], "You open Your hand and satisfy every living thing [with] *desire*," "according to *his will and desire*." And since his will and desire is that he should have a desire to study, they will give him a desire to study, because the essence is that he should study,

[And similar to what is explained in the *Maamar* of the Rebbe Rayatz[74] regarding the departure from evil—that it does not matter in what way the matter will be, because the essence is that there should be a departure from evil],

but, after a month, two months, half a year passes, and he will study out of desire—*he will lack* the matter of toil out of *kabbalas ol*, and then he will complain... about the fact that he has a desire (like the aforementioned story regarding the Tzemach Tzedek).

**N.** And in all this is added a special emphasis regarding the students of the Yeshiva who came from our Holy Land, may it be rebuilt speedily[75]:

Among the only things for which it is permitted to leave Eretz Yisrael (and were it not for this, it is forbidden to leave Eretz Yisrael) to go abroad, is "*to study Torah*"[76]. And in this case, the Rebbe took upon himself the responsibility and instructed them to leave Eretz Yisrael in order to study Torah. And since this is so, if they lack, G-d forbid, in the study of Torah, it turns out that the permission for the departure from Eretz Yisrael is not in effect according to law.

#### 93

**O.** And another matter regarding the feeling of responsibility toward the Rebbe, which is equal for all—all the students of the *Temimim*, both from Eretz Yisrael and from abroad, and for all who are connected to the Rebbe:

The story is known[77] regarding our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe, that when they came to arrest him, he hid at first, saying that one must fulfill what is written[78], "Hide for a brief moment until the wrath passes." And when they came the second time, the Chossid R' Shmuel Munkes[79] advised our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe not to hide but to go with them. And when our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe said that there is danger in the matter, R' Shmuel Munkes replied to him: "Either way, if you are a 'Rebbe'—they will not be able to harm you, and if not—is it possible that you have taken from thousands of Jews the '*geshmak*' in physicality?"[80]!...

The Rebbe took—or at least diminished—from thousands of Jews the "*geshmak*" in physicality, for what? For the sake that they might be able to reach the knowledge of Elokus, "Know the G-d of your father, etc."[81]. And consequently, if the conduct is in a way that the purpose of the intent is not fulfilled, the taking of the "*geshmak*" etc. remains without the result.

**P.** In general, one must know that the conduct of Chassidim also pertains to the Rebbe.

In the *Maamar* of the Rebbe (Rashab), may his soul be bound in the bond of life, which he said on the last 20th of Mar-Cheshvan in his lifetime in this world[82], on the *d'h* *Semuchim La'ad*[83] *Parshas* [84], he brings the statement of the Zohar[85], "But *samach* (support)—this is *ezer* (help)," meaning, that generally there is a difference between *samach* and *ezer*, that *samach* is that he supports him so that he will not fall further, and *ezer* is that a renewal is effected in him through the help and assistance. But here the Zohar explains that the support is like help, that the lower supports the higher.

#### 94

And the concept here regarding us—that the mode of our conduct (the conduct of the lower) also pertains to the Rebbe (the higher).

And as is known[86] the story regarding the Chossid R' Y.M. of Poltava[87] who entered for *yechidus* to the Rebbe Maharash, and the Rebbe Maharash said to him: "When there is a Rebbe there are Chassidim, but, Chassidim who are occupied with *avodah*." And he concluded: "Chassidim, when they were Chassidim and men of action in *avodah*, they would strengthen the Rebbe"[88].

From this is understood the greatness of the responsibility of the Chassidim who are connected to the Rebbe, because their conduct also pertains to the Rebbe. Thus, when one conducts himself in an undesirable way, G-d forbid, this is not only in the category of "one who injures himself," who is not permitted but is exempt, but also in the category of "one who injures others," for which he is liable[89]. And all the more so in this case, where the "others" is a general soul, etc., etc., where the responsibility is greatest.

***

**Q.** Regarding the verse "And the life of Sarah was one hundred years and twenty years and seven years" at the beginning of this week's *parsha*—our Sages expounded[90]: "Therefore the word 'year' is written after each total, to tell you that each one is expounded by itself: one hundred like twenty... and twenty like seven."

And one must explain the allusion in this regarding the *avodah* of man:

One hundred years—is the time of the completion and perfection of the *avodah* in all ten powers of the soul as each one of them includes and is detailed into ten, the totality of all of which is the number one hundred[91].

Twenty years—is the time in which the *avodah* begins as a true adult who has *mochin d'gadlus* (as is known that in the law of *katnus* there are different times, and true *gadlus* is effected at the age of twenty years). Therefore, he cannot sell his father's property until he is twenty years old[92]. And likewise, our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe rules in the Laws of Torah Study[93] that at the age of twenty years there are already (not only five years in Scripture

#### 95

and five years in Mishnah, but) also five years in Talmud, and then is the time to pursue ("a twenty-year-old to pursue"[94]) after livelihood.

— The reason for the law that a twenty-year-old must pursue after physical livelihood is because such is the case in spirituality (as discussed many times[95] that the laws in the revealed Torah are taken from the inner dimension of the Torah) regarding spiritual livelihood. And similar to what is explained in Chassidus[96] that since the soul yearns for the spark of holiness that is found in the food, a yearning for the food is also created in the body.

And seven years—is the time of the beginning of education[97].

Based on this, one can explain the allusion of "one hundred like twenty and twenty like seven"—that in order to come to the perfection of the *avodah* of "one hundred years" in a way of "one hundred like twenty," that even at the completion of the *avodah* it will be "without sin" as at the beginning of the *avodah*, there must first be "twenty like seven," that even when he is twenty years old, at which time *mochin d'gadlus* are effected in him, he should not have opinions of his own ("*aigene deios*"), but should conduct himself like a seven-year-old who has no opinions of his own, and does what he is commanded.

Even one who is twenty years old, and has [or at least should have, and therefore thinks he has] *mochin d'gadlus*—he should not make ways of his own ("*aigene derech*"). Each one must estimate for himself—but *aliba d'nafshih* (according to his own self)—what matter the Rebbe would demand of him, and this matter he must fulfill and complete.

***

**R.** (Toward the conclusion of the *farbrengen*, the Rebbe *shlita* said:)

A Chassidic *farbrengen* is a matter of *scent* (as the words of the Rebbe Rayatz[98]), at the time of the act one enjoys the scent ("*m'git a shmek*"), this one enjoys that he heard "*a gut vort*," this one enjoys from the removal of a difficulty in some matter, and the like, and afterward nothing remains regarding action. The intent is that it should not remain in a way of scent only, but that there should also be a way of eating and drinking, meaning, that it should come to actual practice.

The time is already close to two, and soon they will go home to eat "*cholent*"... and will forget about what was discussed.

#### 96

— The Shabbos meal does not effect materialization, as is known that on Yom Tov it is written[99] "the feast of your festivals," "the feast of your festivals it says, and not the feast of your Shabbos"[100] (as explained in *Torah Or* in our *parsha*[101]). And furthermore, this is a *Mitzvah*, and consequently one must fulfill it *with beauty* (*hiddur*), as the language of the Rambam[102], "a dish that is very oily and a perfumed drink." And how good it is in a *double* way, as it is stated in the Midrash[103] that "all the business of Shabbos is double." — The Rebbe Rayatz once related[104] regarding the custom to eat on Shabbos two types of fish, two types of meat, etc. — And consequently, one can be completely immersed ("*ingantzen areingeton*") in the Shabbos meal, saying that he has

## 97

The words of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, in his letter regarding the obligation of *all Bnei Yisrael* to study the teachings of Chassidus, require no further explanation. The connection between the inspiration to study *Da"Ch* (Divrei Elokus Chassidim) and the day of 19 Kislev is also understood, as this day is the Rosh Hashanah of Chassidus[1](#_ftn527), and it is "a time auspicious for strengthening and encouraging oneself in the study of the revealed Torah and *Da"Ch*, for dedicating oneself to the *avodah* of *tefillah*, and for awakening to the refinement and purification of one’s *midos* according to Chassidus, and for *ahavas re'im* without limit"[2](#_ftn528).

Even more understandable is the connection between the dissemination of *Da"Ch* study among all circles of our brethren, the Children of Israel, and the matter of 19 Kislev—based on the words of the Rebbe (Rashab), of blessed memory, that "the essence of *yifutzu mayanosecha chutzah*[3](#_ftn529) began after Petersburg. Before [the teachings of] Chassidus, not every Jew could grasp *Elokus*. Furthermore, one needed to possess a lofty *neshamah*. Or, for one who did not have a lofty *neshamah*, it was only through fixing all the matters that required fixing and refining himself. Chassidus brought about that *every Jew* would have the power to understand a concept of *Elokus* and grasp it [thoroughly]. This began primarily after Petersburg specifically. For after Petersburg, he (the Alter Rebbe) ascended to a higher *madregah*"[4](#_ftn530).

***

#### 98

Regarding the Baal Shem Tov’s question of when [Moshiach] will come—Moshiach was precise in his answer by quoting the verse (Mishlei 5:16): when "your *mayanos* (springs) will spread *chutzah* (outward)." It is known that there is nothing higher than spring water; wherever it flows, even to a distant place, it retains the status of a spring, provided that its connection—its *hiskashrus*—to its source is not severed. For spring water that has been disconnected no longer holds the status of a spring[5](#_ftn531).

All these things demand of each and every one of us: 1) To *disseminate*, 2) The *mayanos*, 3) Even *chutzah*. And all of this is impossible except through an unceasing connection to the source—a connection that grows ever stronger with the Nasi, my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, the source of the dissemination of these *mayanos* to all corners of the world.

May our eyes behold the fulfillment of the promise of Moshiach Tzidkeinu, when "all the *klipos* will cease and it will be a time of *ratzon* and salvation," and may he redeem us with the true and complete Geulah.

**Menachem Schneerson**

*Rosh Chodesh Kislev, 5711*

*Brooklyn, N.Y.*

## With the help of Hashem. Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Vayeitzei, 9 Kislev 5711

#### 99

(Unedited)

**1.** It is a known custom that on the Shabbos before the wedding, the chassan is called up to the Torah.

The reason for this custom—as explained by my father-in-law, the Rebbe—is that as a prerequisite for any undertaking, a Yid must connect with the Torah. This is all the more so when it concerns the most comprehensive undertaking of building a home in Yisrael, a home that will endure forever, founded upon the pillars of Torah and mitzvos. Certainly, as a prerequisite for this, one must connect with the Torah, and this is the concept of the *aliyah* to the Torah on the Shabbos before the wedding.

We must understand why this connection with the Torah, as a prerequisite and preparation for marriage, is specifically through an *aliyah* to the Torah—a connection with the *letters of the Torah Shebichtav* (Written Torah) specifically.

By way of introduction: The Torah is divided into several parts, beginning with the general division into Torah Shebichtav and Torah Shebaal Peh (Oral Torah). Within these, there are further distinctions: In Torah Shebichtav, there is the recitation of the verses of the Torah even without understanding the meaning, the study of the verses of the Torah with their simple meaning, and study with the commentaries of the Torah. In Torah Shebaal Peh, there is the study of the laws and halachos of the Torah, and the study of the reasons for the halachos and the pilpul (analytical depth) of the Torah.

[To add: The distinction between the halachos of the Torah and the pilpul of the Torah is emphasized regarding the obligation to prioritize Torah study *before the wedding*:

The difference between pilpul and halachos is that the pilpul of the Torah is without limit—"there is no end or limit to the depth of the reasons for the halachos and the pilpul regarding them, etc.," and of this it is written, "It is longer than the earth in measure and broader than the sea" [and therefore the obligation of Torah study is a constant obligation at all times, because no matter how much one studies, one can always add and study more, since the Torah is without limit]. In contrast, the halachos of the Torah have a limit, an end, and a boundary [and therefore it is possible to study the entire Torah, as we find in the Gemara, "Ben Dama asked... what about one like me, who has studied the entire Torah?"—the meaning of "I have studied the entire Torah" (for seemingly, how is it possible to study the entire Torah, which is without limit?) is the study of all the *halachos* of the Torah, which have a limit].

This distinction is emphasized regarding the obligation to prioritize Torah study before the wedding: The study of halachos takes precedence over all other matters, to the point that "it overrides the great mitzvah of procreation, even though it is greater than all the mitzvos... it is overridden by the mitzvah of Talmud Torah, which is weighed against all the mitzvos." In contrast, the study of pilpul, even though there is an obligation in this study [for even the pilpul of the Torah that was given to Moshe, "Moshe acted with a generous eye and gave it to Yisrael"], it does not take precedence over all matters, and it is not to be set aside for the sake of procreation (as our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe writes in Hilchos Talmud Torah, and he elaborates on this in the Kuntres Acharon)].

We must understand: What is the reason that the connection with the Torah as a preparation and prerequisite for marriage is through an *aliyah* to the Torah, where one reads *specifically from the Torah Shebichtav*, and within Torah Shebichtav itself, one reads *the letters of the Torah* specifically, without any translation at all, not even the simple meaning ("the literal translation") as one learns in "cheder"?!

**2.** This can be connected to what is related in the parsha of the week regarding the conduct of Yaakov concerning the matter of marriage—when he prepared to establish and build the entire house of Yisrael:

By way of introduction: The conduct of Yaakov is the conduct of Yisrael in their purity, as they are set apart for themselves. In the words of Chazal, "From Avraham came Yishmael, etc.; from Yitzchak came Esav," whereas "Yaakov’s bed was complete."

Regarding the conduct of Yaakov, the Torah relates many details. All the details in this narrative—like all the stories of the Torah—constitute a lesson and instruction for all generations, in every place and at every time, including our own days, for the Torah is eternal.

It begins with what is related at the beginning of the parsha: "Yaakov departed from Beer Sheva and went to Charan"—a matter already mentioned at the end of the previous parsha, and repeated at the beginning of our parsha (as Rashi explains, "he returned to the first subject"). From this, we understand the magnitude of the emphasis on this matter. For all matters of the Torah are with the utmost precision, to the point that even from the redundancy of a single letter, one learns several halachos, and all the more so when an entire subject is repeated; certainly, there is a lesson and instruction for the generations here, as follows.

**3.** "Yaakov departed from Beer Sheva and went to Charan":

Regarding the name "Beer Sheva," we find two reasons in the Torah: (a) Named after the oath (*shevuah*) made at the time of the covenant between Avraham and Avimelech; (b) Named after the seventh well (*beer*) that the servants of Yitzchak dug after the peace with Avimelech. Both reasons point to a state of *rest and tranquility* for Bnei Yisrael.

In contrast, the name "Charan" points to an *opposite* state—as Chazal explain, "Charan" points to the "wrath (*charon*) of the world."

And yet, it is said, "Yaakov departed from Beer Sheva and went to Charan"—a departure from a place and state of rest and tranquility to a place and state of the "wrath of the world."

From here, we find an answer to a general question:

Since Hashem has given us Torah and mitzvos from His full and wide hand, and at every turn ("wherever one turns") we must fulfill one of the 248 positive mitzvos and their branches, or at the very least refrain from one of the 365 negative mitzvos and their branches—He should have, at the very least, removed from us our worries: the worry of galus, the worry of livelihood, and the like, so that it would be easier to fulfill the mitzvos? And furthermore: It would have been better if we did not have to deal with worldly matters at all, but rather remain in the "tent of Torah"?

To this, the Torah tells us of the conduct of Yaakov—that when he stood to establish the house of Yisrael, he was commanded to depart from Beer Sheva, to depart even from the yeshivos of Shem and Ever, and to go to Charan, the "wrath of the world":

Before he needed to establish the people of Yisrael, he could and should have been in "Beer Sheva," a place of manifest revelation of Elokus, where the fulfillment of mitzvos is possible with ease and transgressions with difficulty.

But in order to build the house of Yisrael, he had to depart from Beer Sheva and go to Charan, a place where Elokus is in concealment and hiding [for even the word "olam" (world) is—as its name suggests—from the root of *he'elem* (concealment), and all the more so "the wrath of the world"], where transgressions are possible with greater ease and mitzvos with great difficulty. And specifically there—by standing up to the tests—he merited to build the house of Yisrael, "his bed was complete."

This is the instruction for every Yid—that specifically through having tests, and overcoming them and standing firm in them, one merits to build a Jewish home, a home that is illuminated and a home that is warm.

**4.** The text continues with the order of Yaakov’s journey to Charan—the first thing he did when going to Charan was "he encountered the place," which is an expression of prayer:

Upon departing for Charan to find his match, he should have, seemingly, learned the language and customs of the land, dressed in the clothing of the land, etc. And yet, Yaakov set aside all these matters, and what did he occupy himself with? The avodah of prayer.

The instruction from this:

When a Yid prepares to build a home in Yisrael, he might think: It is fine that until now his occupation was in the study of Torah, the fulfillment of mitzvos, and prayer, but now, as he goes out into the world, he must set everything aside and devote himself to learning and imitating the customs of the land, pursuing the inhabitants of the land, and the like.

To this comes the instruction from the conduct of Yaakov—"he encountered the place"—that specifically now he must devote himself even more to the avodah of prayer. For his previous avodah, in which he occupied himself with Torah, mitzvos, and prayer, is not enough for his current state, as he goes out into the world, where he must stand up to great tests that are incomparable to the previous ones. Therefore, he must pray and ask Hashem that he should stand up to the test.

**5.** The text continues—"He took from the stones of the place and placed them around his head," and Rashi explains, "He made them like a gutter around his head because he was afraid of wild beasts":

Seemingly, the reason is not understood why he "made them like a gutter around *his head*" only, and not also around all his other limbs—for either way: If he had trust that Hashem would protect him, he should not have made a gutter even around his head. And if he did not want to rely on matters that are above nature, he should have made a gutter around his body and feet as well?

This can be understood by first explaining the precision in the language of the verse, "When you eat the labor of *your hands*, you are fortunate"—"*your hands*" specifically—that the occupation and labor in livelihood should be (not with the head, "the labor of your head," but) with *the hands* ("your hands") only, while the head remains free, placed in Torah and avodah. And then (when "the labor of *your hands* (only) you shall eat," but the head is placed in Torah and avodah)—"*you are fortunate and it is good for you*" (as the verse concludes), and not only in spirituality, but also in physicality. For the abundance of cleverness and schemes in matters of livelihood ("the labor of your head"), not only is there no benefit in them, but on the contrary, they cause more damage, as it is written, "It is not for the wise to have bread" (as explained in many places).

Regarding our subject:

Upon departing for Charan, Yaakov knew well to what place he was going, and he knew that there he would have to work for his livelihood for Lavan the Aramean (the burdens of livelihood), and he knew that even before his arrival in Charan, while he was on the way, there were wild beasts (burdens even in the preparations for livelihood). And in order to protect himself so that the departure into the world would not affect him, he made a "gutter around *his head*"—to protect *the head* so that nothing would strike it ("that no one should trample upon the head"), so that his occupation in livelihood (his work in the house of Lavan) and the preparation for it (the journey on the way) would be in the manner of "the labor of *your hands*" only, while his head would remain placed in Torah and avodah.

This is the instruction for every Yid—that upon going out into the world, one must be careful that *the head* is protected, by having it placed in Torah and avodah. And when the head is as it should be, then automatically the hands and feet will also be as they should be; the business of livelihood will be according to the Shulchan Aruch, the conduct of the hands will be in a manner that "the left (only) pushes away (but) the right draws near," and the feet will run to perform a mitzvah.

**6.** And this matter ("a gutter around his head") Yaakov did with "*the stones* of the place"—*stones* specifically:

Stones are a type of *domem* (inanimate), which have no life, and do not even have the soul of the plant. Their concept in avodah is *kabbalas ol* (acceptance of the yoke), without intellect and without emotion.

The point of this is that in order for the departure into the world not to affect him, intellect and emotion are not enough; one needs a complete *kabbalas ol malchus shamayim*, the concept of *bitul*, that he feels himself like an inanimate stone, as it is written, "If I have not calmed and quieted my soul," "and my soul shall be as dust to all."

*Domem* cannot move by itself, until someone takes it and moves it from place to place. And so one must feel himself as *domem*—a simple servant—that Hashem carries him (and one must allow himself to be carried) from place to place in order to fulfill the intention and the mission that was placed upon him.

This is also the content of the continuation of the matter, "And Yaakov vowed a vow, saying, 'If Hashem will be with me, etc.'"—the connection to Hashem through a *vow* specifically—for the concept of a vow is that even if from the perspective of desire, intellect, and emotion he would not do the thing, he is compelled to do it because of the vow, out of *kabbalas ol*.

And the reward for this (as it concludes in the parsha) is "And this stone which I have set as a pillar shall be a house of Hashem"—that even though we are talking about ordinary stones (and certainly not silver and gold), and furthermore, not even stones of a city, stones of a dwelling place, but stones found in the middle of the road, nevertheless, they too became a "house of Hashem." Its concept in avodah is that even his *ordinary and simple* things (like ordinary stones in the middle of the road) become permeated with holiness, a "*house of Hashem*."

**7.** This is the instruction and moral lesson for all, and especially for a chassan and kallah who are standing to build a home in Yisrael:

A home in Yisrael must be a "house of Hashem," by becoming permeated with holiness, meaning, that not only the Siddur and the Chumash in the home are holy, but also (to distinguish) the fork and the spoon in the home are permeated with holiness.

This is accomplished through the appropriate preparation, beginning with the matter of prayer, "he encountered the place," and also "he took from the stones of the place and placed them around his head," surrounding the head with the matter of *kabbalas ol* (as elaborated above). And through this, he ensures that not only during the time of prayer, and not only while he is awake, but even at the time of sleep ("he lay down in that place") there is a state and condition that "*this*—the place where he is found—is the *gate of heaven*," and the home and all that is in it is a "*house of Hashem*."

Based on this, we can explain the reason that the connection with the Torah on the Shabbos before the wedding is through an *aliyah* to the Torah specifically, reading in the Torah without any translation at all (as mentioned in section 1)—for in this, it is emphasized that the preparation for marriage through the connection with the Torah is (not from the perspective of intellect, study through understanding and comprehension, but) from *kabbalas ol*, which is expressed in the reading of the *letters of the Torah* without any translation at all. And this is the appropriate preparation for building a home in Yisrael in the manner of a "house of Hashem."

[The Rebbe instructed that businessmen who have a set time to study with a *chavrusa* at least once a week should say "L'chaim."]

***

**8.** The timing of Shabbos Parshas Vayeitzei this year is on the 9th of Kislev—the yahrtzeit of the Mitteler Rebbe, and also his birthday, for he passed away on the same day he was born.

In one of his letters, my father-in-law, the Rebbe, explains the greatness of the matter of observing the yahrtzeit of a tzaddik through study and a farbrengen. In it, he relates about the yahrtzeit of the 9th of Kislev in the year 5657, which fell on Shabbos Parshas Vayeitzei (like the timing of this year), and at that time the Rebbe (Rashab) said a maamar on the verse "And this stone which I have set as a pillar shall be a house of Hashem." At the farbrengen that took place then, the Rebbe said: Observing the yahrtzeit through studying the Torah of the one whose yahrtzeit it is, and by holding a farbrengen, is a *pidyon nefesh* (redemption of the soul) that one gives to the one whose yahrtzeit it is.

It is easy to imagine that if it were possible now to enter to the Mitteler Rebbe and give him a pidyon, everyone would certainly rush to enter ("he would not think at all and would immediately go in") and give a pidyon.

And based on what has been said, there is the possibility now to give a pidyon to the Mitteler Rebbe—through the farbrengen that is taking place now, and through the study of his Torah (after the farbrengen, when everyone returns to their home), to study a subject in one of the books of the Mitteler Rebbe, the one whose yahrtzeit it is.

**9.** It should be added and clarified that this matter is not in contradiction, G-d forbid, to the connection to my father-in-law, the Rebbe; we have no one but the Nasi of our generation—for the connection (through giving the pidyon) is to the Mitteler Rebbe as he is included in my father-in-law, the Rebbe, the Nasi of our generation.

I once related that when they were arranging the title page for the printed Chassidus books, there was a discussion whether to write "Collection of the Chain of the *Light* (*Ohr*)," or "Collection of the Chain of the *Luminary* (*Maor*)," and the conclusion was to write "Collection of the Chain of the *Light*," because in a "Luminary," a "chain" is not applicable, as it is essential.

Regarding our subject:

Each of the Rebbes is a "Luminary," but in the "Luminaries" themselves, each Rebbe has a special matter, in accordance with his level in the Sefiros, as is known that the Baal Shem Tov is Atik, the Maggid is Arich, the Alter Rebbe is Chochma, the Mitteler Rebbe is Binah, etc. And my father-in-law, the Rebbe, the "Luminary" of our generation, includes all the "Luminaries"—the Baal Shem Tov, the Maggid, the Alter Rebbe, the Mitteler Rebbe, the Tzemach Tzedek, the Rebbe Maharash, and the Rebbe (Rashab)—for he carries out all the matters that they carried out in their time.

Consequently, we must now connect to the "Luminary" of the Mitteler Rebbe as he is included in the "Luminary" of our generation, my father-in-law, the Rebbe.

**10.** Continuing from what was said that the farbrengen on the day of the yahrtzeit is the pidyon that one gives to the one whose yahrtzeit it is—it is worthwhile and appropriate that they should now play (at the farbrengen of the yahrtzeit) a nigun attributed to the Chassidim of the Mitteler Rebbe.

Among the nigunim attributed to the Chassidim of the Mitteler Rebbe is the nigun "Nie Zhuritzi Chlopzi," and the Rebbe (Rashab) explains that for the Chassidim of the Mitteler Rebbe, the only worry was how to come to the "inn" ("yak dayedem da kartshamke"), and when they arrived there, then nothing was lacking...

The content of the matter:

When one is traveling on the road, one enters the "inns" ("kretshmes") that are found in the middle of the road, and there one can rest, pray, and study the lessons with peace of mind.

The analogy to this is that the journey on the road refers to the time of galus, "because of our sins we were exiled from our land," that all Bnei Yisrael (both those who are here, in the diaspora, and those who are in Eretz Yisrael) are in galus, on the road, and in the middle of the road there are "inns" ("kretshmes"), which are the places of the *Rebbes*.

Consequently, one must strive to minimize as much as possible the stay on the road, and to be found more in the "inn" ("kretshme"), in the place of the *Rebbe*, with the knowledge that there, nothing is lacking.

And as mentioned, they should now play this nigun (certainly everyone knows the nigun).

[They began to play the nigun, and the Rebbe said: Why are you playing just to fulfill the obligation? Play with more vitality. Afterwards, he stopped again and said that they should play with more vitality. And they played with great arousal, and the Rebbe also played with the crowd and swayed strongly with his hand.]

***

**11.** Continuing from what was said about giving a pidyon (through study and the farbrengen), it should be added that giving a pidyon is not only regarding spiritual matters, but also regarding physical matters. For although the Alter Rebbe in Iggeres HaKodesh rejects the conduct of "asking for physical advice as to what to do in matters of the physical world," because this is a matter that belongs only to "true prophets," nevertheless, Chassidim were not impressed by this... and they continued to ask also regarding physical matters, and this is how even the greatest Chassidim conducted themselves.

And to add: After knowing what the Alter Rebbe said, that an answer regarding physical matters belongs to "true prophets," then we know that the answer of the Rebbe regarding physical matters is because he is a "*true prophet*!"

Chassidim are not afraid. If there is a need for the Rebbe to be a prophet, then he is a prophet; the main thing is that he should give an answer to the matters that Chassidim ask and request of him.

Regarding our subject:

Since a farbrengen was held in connection with the yahrtzeit, and in it they also played a nigun attributed to the Chassidim of the Mitteler Rebbe, and everyone took upon themselves to study a subject in one of the books of the Mitteler Rebbe—this is a pidyon given so that the Rebbe will help not only in spirituality, but also in physicality.

And although now the Rebbe is in a state of separation ("departed") from the world, nevertheless, he will influence also physicality, and will give the strength to make of the physicality a "house of Hashem," in the aspect of "And this stone which I have set as a pillar shall be a house of Hashem."

**12.** This matter belongs especially to what was discussed above (section 7) regarding chassanim and kallos who are standing to establish a home in Yisrael—that they have a special empowerment from the Rebbe (as a result of the aforementioned pidyon) that their home should be a "house of Hashem," a Jewish home, a warm home, and an illuminated home.

A Jewish home must also be a warm home (for there can be a Jewish home that is a cold home, just as there can be a Yid who is cold), and it must also be an illuminated home, "a bright home," a home that illuminates the entire environment.

And the strength for this is through the *pnimiyus* (inner dimension) of the Torah, which is the Luminary in the Torah:

It is explained in Torah Or that the *tzimtzum* (contraction) is only in the light, but in the Luminary, *tzimtzum* is not applicable.

From this, it is understood also regarding the Luminary in the Torah, the *pnimiyus* of the Torah—that limitations are not applicable to it, and through it one can illuminate the entire environment, which is the concept of "may your springs spread outward."

And this matter belongs especially to the one whose yahrtzeit it is, the Mitteler Rebbe—as is known that even during his father's lifetime, he began to say Chassidus in his own style, with length of explanation, etc., and when they related this to the Alter Rebbe, he said: Each generation and its expositors; he—the Mitteler Rebbe—must say Chassidus in such a manner, and through him will be fulfilled the prophecy "may your springs spread outward."

## B"H, 10 Kislev, 5711

#### 110

Brooklyn, N.Y.

To Anash, and to all lovers of Torah
Wherever they may be,
May Hashem grant them life,

Peace and blessings!

The day of tidings is drawing near, Tuesday, the 19th of Kislev—the day upon which the righteousness of the Alter Rebbe (author of the Tanya and Shulchan Aruch) was revealed, and with it, the righteousness of the teachings of Chassidus: the teachings of Chabad Chassidus and the teachings of Chassidus in general.

My revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, wrote in his letter of 10 Kislev, 5710—two months before his passing—that "the study of the teachings of Chassidus is not a matter pertaining only to any particular group; rather, the obligation to study it is incumbent upon all of Klal Yisrael, and upon those who study Torah in particular." And it is an obligation upon Anash to be *engaged* in the dissemination of the study of Chassidus. And "engagement" means a ceaseless effort, such that in all of one's affairs—even the peripheral ones—one intends to achieve this goal, "just as in business, where one is occupied with it all day long, not necessarily only when one is engaged in the core of the business, because the matter is of the utmost importance to him. He is immersed in it at every hour and at every time."

In another place, it will be explained at length how the condition that "your springs shall be dispersed *outward*"—of the Baal Shem Tov—is a prerequisite, a preparation, and a vessel for the coming of Moshiach.

#### 111

This condition, which each and every one of us must strive with all our might to fulfill, contains three details:

(a) *Shall be dispersed (Yafutzu).* To teach the study of Chassidus in a manner of *dissemination*—spreading it over a wide area, so that the study reaches all parts of the place where one teaches this Torah.

(b) *Your springs (Ma'ayanosecha).* The teachings of Chassidus that one disseminates must retain the qualities of a spring. There are two primary qualities in a spring: 1) Its waters are living and flow ceaselessly from the source of the spring. 2) One does not pay so much attention to quantity, for the spring flows from its source drop by drop. This is also the condition for the dissemination of this Torah. Quantity is not the main thing, provided that the study is alive with the vitality of the soul and internal enthusiasm, and that it flows from its source—from the inner soul of the one disseminating the Torah, who is connected in the depths of his heart to the Nesi'im of the teachings of Chassidus.

(c) *Outward (Chutzah).* We must not be satisfied with studying only in the beis medrash and the shul, but also "when you walk by the way, when you sit in your shop," and the like. Similarly, regarding the nature of the students, one should not be limited to certain groups or specific types of people, for the obligation to study the teachings of Chassidus is incumbent even upon the simplest of the simple, just as even the greatest of the great cannot reach its ultimate end.

And even for those who are found outside of all camps, even in a place that the verse testifies is *outward*—even there, the springs must be disseminated.

And the merit of the effort in disseminating the springs outward will draw down and serve as a vessel to receive the blessings of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, who blessed each and every one of us on the 19th of Kislev before his passing, and surely blesses us now as well for the 19th of Kislev of this year, saying to us directly: "May you be blessed by Hashem with a good year in the study and dissemination of Chassidus; you, your households, your sons and daughters, may they live in the ways of Chassidus; and you, your households, and all who are joined to you, may you all be blessed with everything you need, in both material and spiritual matters."

With blessings for success in this avodah and all the best,

**Menachem Schneerson**

## B"H. The text of the telegram that the Rebbe shlita was pleased to send to Anash, may they live, throughout the world in anticipation of 19 Kislev, 5711

#### 112

I request that Anash gather for a farbrengen on 19 Kislev and on the upcoming fifth night of Chanukah, while making an effort to also include those who are not Anash, and to organize the dissemination of the wellsprings of Chassidus outward.

**Menachem Schneerson**

## Sicha of 19 Kislev, 5711

#### 113

**A.** It is well known that all matters below have a root above, from which they are branched out downwards, as the statement says, "The Holy One, blessed be He, made the lower world in the likeness of the upper world," and "The kingdom of the earth is like the kingdom of the heavens." From this, it is understood regarding the imprisonment and redemption of our Rebbe, the author of the Tanya and Shulchan Aruch—for which 19 Kislev was established as a permanent festival (until the coming of Moshiach, and even thereafter)—that the primary reason for this is because these matters existed above, and from there they were branched out below.

By way of introduction, consider the story that during the time our Rebbe was sitting in imprisonment in the Peter and Paul Fortress, his teachers—his teacher, the Maggid, and his teacher’s teacher, the Baal Shem Tov—came to him from the World of Truth.

—It should be added that their coming was *clothed in bodies*. The Rebbe, our master and teacher, once visited this specific place, and when he returned from there, his father, the Rebbe, asked him if this room *could contain three people*! This question proves that their coming was in a state of being clothed in bodies. And it is known that the revelation of the soul as it comes clothed in a body is a *higher revelation* than the revelation of the soul without a body—

During the exchange of words (the lengthy conversation) that took place between them, our Rebbe asked them what was the reason for his sitting in prison. They replied that the accusation against him had intensified because he was revealing words of Chassidus—the secrets of the Torah, the crown of the King—in public. He asked them how he should conduct himself after he was released from prison: should he change, or continue his path of revealing Chassidus in public? They replied that since he had begun to reveal Chassidus in public, he must continue and increase even more.

Thus, it is found that the cause of the imprisonment below was a result of the accusation that existed

#### 114

above regarding the revelation of Chassidus in public, which is why an accusation was created below as well. And the reason for the release and redemption was a result of the fact that the accusation above was nullified, and they agreed above with the approach of our Rebbe regarding the revelation and dissemination of the teachings of Chassidus in public.

This is the content of the festival of 19 Kislev—*Didan Notzach* (We have won)—not only the victory over the Russian government, but also, and primarily, the *victory above*, that the approach of our Rebbe, that one must reveal and disseminate the teachings of Chassidus in public, became an accepted approach and a definitive ruling.

**B.** More specifically, the victory of our Rebbe’s approach is expressed in two things:

1) The relevance of the teachings of Chassidus to *all of Israel*.

—Before this, the accepted approach was that the study of the secrets of the Torah belonged only to the select few, or at the very least to those who had already rectified all the matters requiring rectification. Our Rebbe innovated that the study of Chassidus belongs to all of Israel, even to the lowest souls in the footsteps of Moshiach, and even those who have not yet fulfilled their role in "do good," and even those who are not yet complete in "turn away from evil"—they, too, belong to the study of Chassidus.

In the words of the Rebbe (Rashab): After Petersburg (after the redemption of our Rebbe), the main matter of "may your springs be dispersed outward" began, since then it was accepted above that our Rebbe’s approach was to reveal and disseminate the teachings of Chassidus to all of Israel.

2) The study of the teachings of Chassidus (by all of Israel) in a manner of *understanding and comprehension*, like the study of a topic in the revealed Torah.

—Study with understanding and comprehension is specifically a condition in the study of the Oral Torah, as it is known that regarding the Written Torah, "If one utters it with his lips, even if he does not understand the meaning of the words because he is an unlearned person, he fulfills the mitzvah of 'and you shall learn them,' and therefore every unlearned person recites the blessing of the Torah in the morning before the verses, and likewise when he is called to the Torah scroll... But in the Oral Torah, if he does not understand the meaning, it is not considered

#### 115

study at all" (as is the ruling of the Magen Avraham, and our Rebbe brought it); and the innovation is that even the study of Chassidus—the secrets of the Torah, Kabbalah, which belongs to the *Written* Torah—can and must be in a manner of *understanding and comprehension*.

In the words of the Rebbe (Rashab): After Petersburg, the teachings of Chassidus were clothed *in intellect*, in a way that even the intellect clothed in the physical brain can understand Elokus.

This is the content of the festival of 19 Kislev—*Didan Notzach*—that our Rebbe’s approach was accepted, that the teachings of Chassidus belong to *all of Israel*, and every Jew can *learn and understand with his intellect* the matters of the teachings of Chassidus.

And this is the preparation for the coming of Moshiach our righteous one—as Moshiach said to the Baal Shem Tov in response to his question, "When will the Master come?"—"When your springs are dispersed outward."

**C.** And after 19 Kislev—when our Rebbe’s approach was accepted that every Jew belongs to learning and understanding the teachings of Chassidus—it became a *holy obligation* upon every Jew to learn Chassidus. That is to say, the study of Chassidus is not something that depends on his will and choice, but rather, like all matters of the Torah, there is an *obligation* to learn them.

Furthermore:

The study of Torah must be immediately *at the beginning of the day*. Therefore, in the morning blessings, we recite the blessing of the Torah, and we say verses from the Written Torah (the verses of the Priestly Blessing), and Mishnah from the Oral Torah. And according to our custom to recite the morning blessings (including the blessing of the Torah) before putting on the tallis and tefillin, it is found that the study of Torah must be even *before the fulfillment of the mitzvos*.

And in these days—before the coming of Moshiach, when "many will be purified, whitened, and refined"—it must be so also in the study of Chassidus, that immediately at the beginning of the day, before putting on the tallis and tefillin, one must learn Chassidus.

Seemingly, there is room for an argument: It is well and good to study the Kitzur Shulchan Aruch—one cannot delay it, since one needs to know the practical laws; but the study of

#### 116

Chassidus, the inner dimension of the Torah—one can delay until after he has filled his belly with Shas and Poskim, and at the very least until his service in "turn away from evil and do good" is as it should be. The answer to this: Just as the obligation of studying the revealed Torah is immediately at the beginning of the day, even before putting on the tallis and tefillin, so too the obligation of studying the teachings of Chassidus is immediately at the beginning of the day, without regard to his standing and situation in "turn away from evil and do good" (before putting on the tallis and tefillin).

And "it is not in heaven"—since after Petersburg, the main matter of "may your springs be dispersed outward" began, that the springs of the Baal Shem Tov and the Maggid, as they were explained in the teachings of our Rebbe, come in a manner of dissemination even to the outside, that is, to every Jew without regard to his standing and situation.

**D.** And since 19 Kislev is the "New Year for Chassidus," this is the appropriate time for everyone to take upon themselves a good resolution to set times for the study of Chassidus.

—Regarding the study of the revealed Torah, certainly everyone has set times, and in addition to this, everyone must take upon themselves to have set times for the study of Chassidus.

And there is an added virtue in making a resolution regarding the study of Chassidus *on 19 Kislev*—because it is the "*New Year* for Chassidus," "New Year" specifically, like the head that leads the entire body, and according to the decision of the head, the entire body behaves (when the body is healthy). Certainly, the resolutions made on it *will be fulfilled in practice* throughout the year.

**E.** And through this, one merits the fulfillment of the promise, "If you walk in My statutes... and I will give your rains in their time":

"In My statutes you shall walk"—Rashi explains—"that you should be *toiling* in the Torah."

And the main matter of toil and labor in the Torah is *in the inner dimension of the Torah*—as the Rebbe explains that the study of Chassidus is a study of the utmost depth, for all the topics in this Torah are subtle intellects in very deep logical arguments. Not only are the matters studied in the topics of this Torah spiritual in their essence, but even the explanations, examples, and parables are from matters that are removed from the senses of touch and the tangible, and what is grasped in a physical perception, such as the powers of the soul, and the like. Therefore, there is a need for *the utmost toil and labor*.
